بالمرئي من غير انطباع الصورة في الحدقة والطبيعيين يقولون : بأن الخروج متوهم والرؤية بالانطباع وتفصيله فيما سيأتي في بحث الإدراك بالبصر. قوله بحسب صغر زاوية رأس المخروط سواء كانت الزاوية متحققة أو متوهمة، والصورة منطبعة عندها أولاً قيل : كونه على هيئة المخروط المخصوص من الأصول الموضوعة للمناظر، وقد برهن عليه بعضهم وجعله من مسائل الفن وفيه تأمل . قوله : ( على هيئة مخروط) المخروط شكل مجسم تحيط به دائرة هي قاعدته، وسطح مستدير يقع منها على التضايف إلى نقطة رأسه فإن قلت : إذا كان المرئي مستديراً فظاهر أن المخروط مستدير وأما إذا كان مضلعاً فينبغي أن يكون المخروط أيضاً مضلعاً بناء على أن قائمته تنطبق على سطح المرئي قلت : لا يجب أن تنطبق قاعدته على سطح المرئي بل هي تشتمل على سطح المرئي وعلى أمور أخر غيره، نعم المخروط الصغير الداخل في المخروط الأول الحاصل من الخطوط الواصلة إلى سطح المرئي يكون تابعاً لسطح المرئي إن كان مضلعاً فمضلع، وإن كان مستديرا فمستدير قوله : (بحسب صغر زاوية رأس المخروط كلامه يدل صريحاً على حدوث زاوية الرؤية عند الحدقة وهو المشهور لكن لا يكفي في الإبصار الانطباع في الجليدية، وإلا يرى شيء واحد شيئين لانطباع صورته في جليدتي العينين بل لا بد من تأدي الصورة إلى العصبتين المجوفتين وإلى الحس المشترك، لا بمعنى انتقالها إليها إذا لا يجوز انتقال العرض بل بمعنى أن انطباعها الجليدية معد لفيضان الصورة على الملتقى وفيضانها عليه معد لفيضانها على الحس المشترك كما أن مقابلة المبصر للباصرة توجب استعدادا يفيض به صورته على الجليدية ولوجوب تأدي الصورة إلى الملتقى قال في بيان سبب رؤية الواحد كثيراً: وقعت الخطوط الشعاعية على المرئي من محاذاة واحدة هي ملتقاهما لا لأن زاوية الرؤية تحدث عند الملتقى كما ظن هذا بقي هاهنا بحث هو أن قوله : رؤية الأشياء على القول الخ يشعر بأن سياق كلامه على مذهب الرياضيين، وحديث الزاوية إنما يناسب مذهب الطبيعيين أعني القول بالانطباع أو
As for the second (point), which is the invalidity of considering its judgment regarding particulars, it is because the judgment of sense concerning particulars errs frequently. And if so, then its judgment concerning any particular is prone to error, so it is not acceptable or considered. And we only said it errs frequently (for reasons: First: we see the small as large, like a distant fire in darkness) this is when it is not very distant. The reason is that the air around it is illuminated by its light, and the visual ray aligned with what is around it does not penetrate the darkness completely, so the fire's body is not distinguished by the observer from the illuminated air resembling it in light, so he perceives both together as one whole and considers them fire. And when it is near, the ray penetrates and the fire is distinguished from the adjacent illuminated air, so he perceives it as it is in its smallness. And when it is very distant, it is like distant visible objects whose case will be known. (And like a grape in water appears like a pear). And its cause is that the vision of things, according to the most apparent opinion, is only by the emission of the ray in the form of a circular cone whose vertex is at the pupil and its base on the surface of the visible object, and its size varies small and large according to the smallness and largeness of the angle of the cone's vertex. Then the radial lines on the surface of the cone penetrate the visible object straight to its ends, if the transparent medium between the observer and the visible object is uniform in thickness and thinness. If variation is supposed, such that what is near the observer is thin like air and what is near the visible object is thick like water in our example. His statement: (according to the most apparent opinion) i.e., the most famous among the philosophers, cautioning against the view of a group of them, which is that the transparent thing between the eye and the visible object is conditioned by the quality of the visual ray, and it becomes an instrument for vision, and contrary to what al-Imam held that when the visible object faces the observer in a specific manner, God creates vision without connection of a ray nor impression of an image. His statement: (by emission of the ray) actual or imagined, for they agree that vision is by emission of the ray in the form of a circular cone, except that the mathematicians say that the emission is actual and vision is by the connection of the ray to the visible object without impression of the image in the pupil, while the natural philosophers say that the emission is imagined and vision is by impression, and its detail will come in the discussion of perception by sight. His statement: depending on the smallness of the angle of the cone's vertex, whether the angle is actual or imagined, and the image is impressed at it or not. It is said: its being in the form of a specific cone is one of the principles laid down for optics, and some have demonstrated it and made it among the matters of the art, but there is reflection in it. His statement: (in the form of a cone) the cone is a solid figure surrounded by a circle which is its base, and a curved surface that tapers from it to its vertex. If you say: if the visible object is circular, then it is apparent that the cone is circular, but if it is polygonal, then the cone should also be polygonal based on its lateral surface conforming to the surface of the visible object. I say: its base does not have to conform to the surface of the visible object; rather it includes the surface of the visible object and other things besides it. Yes, the small inner cone within the first cone resulting from the lines reaching the surface of the visible object follows the surface of the visible object: if polygonal then polygonal, if circular then circular. His statement: (depending on the smallness of the angle of the cone's vertex) his words clearly indicate the occurrence of the visual angle at the pupil, which is well-known. However, impression in the crystalline lens is not sufficient for vision; otherwise one thing would be seen as two due to the impression of its image in the two crystalline lenses of the two eyes. Rather, it is necessary that the image be conveyed to the two hollow nerves and to the common sense, not meaning its transfer to them (since transfer of an accident is not permissible) but that its impression in the crystalline lens prepares for the emanation of the image upon the meeting point (المتلقي), and its emanation upon it prepares for its emanation upon the common sense, just as the facing of the visible object to the observer necessitates a preparedness for the emanation of its image upon the crystalline lens. And due to the necessity of conveying the image to the meeting point, he said, in explaining the cause of seeing one thing as many: the radial lines fall upon the visible object from one alignment which is their meeting point, not because the visual angle occurs at the meeting point as was thought. There remains here a discussion: that his statement 'vision of things according to the opinion...' suggests that his context follows the mathematicians' school, while the discussion of the angle is appropriate only to the naturalists' school, i.e., the opinion of impression or...
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage refutes the reliability of sensory judgments even in particulars by showing they frequently err. It begins by stating that sensory judgments in particulars err often, so any particular judgment is suspect. Then it gives two faces (reasons): First, we see a small fire as large when it is distant in darkness, because the visual ray does not penetrate the illuminated air surrounding the fire, so the observer conflates the fire with the glowing air. When the fire is nearer, the ray penetrates and the true size is perceived. Second, a grape in water appears larger like a pear due to refraction when the medium is not uniform (water vs air). The explanation of these errors leads to a detailed discussion of the mechanism of vision. According to the dominant view (of philosophers), vision occurs by a ray emitted in a cone shape from the eye to the object. The size of the visual angle determines perceived size. The passage then discusses different schools: mathematicians hold that the ray actually emits and vision is by contact without image impression; natural philosophers hold that the ray is imagined and vision is by impression of the image in the eye. It also discusses the necessity of the image reaching the common sense via the optic nerves, and clarifies that a single object is seen as one due to the convergence of rays at the meeting point, not because the visual angle originates there. The overall argument is that sensory perception is unreliable due to physical and physiological factors, thus sensory judgments cannot be accepted as certain, whether universal or particular.

Key Terms
مخروط
زاوية الرؤية
انطباع الصورة
الرياضيون
الطبيعيون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All philosophers agree on how vision works.
Correction: This passage shows a major disagreement between mathematicians and natural philosophers, indicating that even experts disagree on the mechanism of sight.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main difference between mathematicians and natural philosophers regarding vision?
A) Mathematicians believe in a cone shape, natural philosophers do not.
B) Mathematicians say the ray actually emits and vision is by contact; natural philosophers say the emission is imagined and vision is by impression.
C) Mathematicians deny the existence of the visual ray, natural philosophers affirm it.
D) Mathematicians focus on angles, natural philosophers focus on medium.

Key Takeaway
The complexity and disagreement in optical theory further support the unreliability of sensory judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The experts themselves disagree on how vision works. How much do you trust your own eyes when they tell you something about Allah's creation? Can you name one area where your senses might be deceiving you about a spiritual truth?
?
The grape illusion proves your eyes can be wrong. What if the 'pear' you are chasing in life—status, wealth, comfort—is actually just a distorted grape? What would it take for you to see through the refraction?
?
If vision itself is such a complex and disputed mechanism, how can you rely on your sight to judge matters of faith? Are you willing to submit your sensory conclusions to the Quran and Sunnah, even when they feel obvious? Commit now: what is one sensory 'certainty' you will reconsider today?

Why Squinting Causes Double Vision: Two Theories
Explains the phenomenon of double vision when squinting and presents two theories of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the anatomical reason for double vision when squinting and the competing theories of normal single vision from the mathematicians and physicists.

Core Idea
Double vision occurs when the two optic nerves misalign, splitting the visual image.

Have you ever squinted and seen two moons? This lesson examines that very phenomenon through medieval Islamic optics. The author first explains the anatomy of vision—how two hollow nerves meet before reaching the eyes—then presents two rival theories about why we normally see one object instead of two. Understanding this debate reveals the sophistication of pre-modern neuroscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the phenomenon
Read the Arabic passage once to become familiar with the phenomenon of double vision when squinting. Note that this is presented as a known fact.
The passage begins with 'you see the one as many, like the moon when squinting.'
2
Understand the anatomical explanation
The author describes the optic nerves (عصبتان مجوفتان) that meet before reaching the eyes. When straight, the visual lines align; when deviated, they misalign, causing double vision. Trace this reasoning.
3
Compare the two theories of normal vision
The mathematicians attribute single vision to simultaneous ray emission; the physicists to a single image at the meeting point. Contrast these explanations.
Both theories account for why one object is seen as one under normal conditions.
4
Reflect on the role of the common sense
The physicists mention that sight is completed by the image at the meeting point and in the common sense (الحس المشترك). Consider why this concept is important.
This distinguishes the physicists' view from a purely optical account.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصلاً ( و) ترى ( الواحد كثيراً كالقمر إذا نظرنا إليه مع غمز إحدى العينين)، وذلك لأن النور البصري يمتد من الدماغ في عصبتين مجوفتين تتلاقيان قبل وصولهما إلى العينين ثم تتباعدان وتتصل كل واحدة منهما بواحدة من العينين، فالعصبتان إذا كانتا مستقيمتين وقعت الخطوط الشعاعية على المرئي من محاذاة واحدة هي ملتقاهما، فيرى واحداً فإذا انحرفتا أو انحرفت إحداهما امتدت تلك الخطوط إلى المرئي من محاذاتين فيرى لذلك اثنين. قوله فيرى (واحداً لوقوع الشعاع الخارج من العينين على المرئي دفعة واحدة عند الرياضيين ولحصول صورة واحدة في الملتقى عند الطبيعيين والإبصار إنما يتم بحصول الصورة فيه بل وفي الحس المشترك لا بمجرد الانطباع في الجليدية وإلا لرئي الشيء الواحد شيئين، فيرى ذلك اثنين لعدم الشعاع من العينين على المرئي دفعة واحدة بل على التعاقب عند الرياضيين وبحصول الصورة في الموضعين من الملتقى لأجل المحاذاتين عند الطبيعيين.
Originally, you see the one as many, like the moon when we look at it with squinting of one eye. That is because the visual light extends from the brain in two hollow nerves that meet before reaching the eyes, then separate, and each connects to one eye. So if the two nerves are straight, the radial lines fall on the seen object from one alignment, which is their meeting point, so it is seen as one. But if they deviate or one of them deviates, those lines extend to the seen object from two alignments, so for that reason it is seen as two. His saying: 'so it is seen as one' due to the rays from both eyes falling on the seen object at once according to the mathematicians, and due to the formation of a single image at the meeting point according to the physicists. Sight is only completed by the formation of the image in it (the meeting point) and in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), not merely by the impression in the crystalline lens; otherwise one thing would be seen as two. So it is seen as two because the rays from the eyes do not fall on the seen object at once but successively according to the mathematicians, and because the image is formed in two places at the meeting point due to the two alignments according to the physicists.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage explains the phenomenon of seeing one object as two when one squints (غمز إحدى العينين). The author first describes the anatomy: the optic nerves (عصبتان مجوفتان) originate from the brain, meet before reaching the eyes, then separate. When the nerves are straight, the visual lines from each eye converge on a single alignment (محاذاة واحدة), so the object is seen as one. If the nerves are deviated, the lines come from two alignments, causing double vision. Then the author presents two theories from the mathematicians (الرياضيون) and physicists (الطبيعيون) to explain why sight normally sees one. The mathematicians say the rays (شعاع بصري) from both eyes strike the object simultaneously, producing a single perception. The physicists say a single image is formed at the meeting point (الملتقى) of the nerves, and this image is conveyed to the common sense (الحس المشترك). If that were not the case, one thing would be seen as two. In the case of squinting, the rays do not strike simultaneously (mathematicians) or the image is formed at two points (physicists), resulting in double vision.

عصبتان مجوفتان
ملتقى
الحس المشترك
شعاع بصري
الرياضيون
الطبيعيون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Double vision is caused by the eye itself, not by the misalignment of the two images at the meeting point.
Correction: The passage explains that double vision results from the deviation of the optic nerves, causing two separate images at the meeting point, not from a problem within the eye.

Checkpoint
According to the physicists, why do we normally see one object as one?
A) Because the rays from both eyes fall on the object simultaneously.
B) Because a single image is formed at the meeting point of the optic nerves.
C) Because the crystalline lens produces a single image.
D) Because the brain ignores the second image.

Key Takeaway
The phenomenon of double vision when squinting reveals the brain's role in merging two images into one, a process explained by two ancient theories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the moon, do you actively remind yourself that your brain is constructing a single image from two streams? How does this awareness affect your trust in your senses as reliable sources of truth?
?
The passage reveals a sophisticated ancient debate about how vision works. Are you humble enough to admit that what you perceive as 'plainly obvious' might be a complex interpretation, not raw reality? How might this humility reshape your intellectual attitude?
?
If even your eyes can be tricked by a slight squint into seeing double, what does that imply about your certainty in other matters you consider self-evident? Will you commit to examining one assumption today that you have always taken for granted?

The Double Moon Illusion: Reflection of Light
Explains the second case of double moon illusion via reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the optical principle of reflection that causes the moon to appear double when near the horizon, and why this only happens at low altitudes.

Core Idea
The moon seems double because direct and reflected rays reach the eye simultaneously near the horizon.

Have you ever noticed the moon appearing as two when it rises over water? This phenomenon is not a split of the moon but a play of light. In this lesson, we explore the second of two cases: how a visual ray from the eye both goes directly to the moon and reflects off water, creating two images. We'll also see why this only works when the moon is low.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation to get a sense of the explanation.
Notice that the passage refers back to a previous case.
2
Identify the two optical rays
Look for the terms 'penetrating ray' (الشعاع النافذ) and 'reflected ray' (الشعاع المنعكس). Understand their paths.
The penetrating ray goes directly to the sky-moon; the reflected ray bounces off water.
3
Understand the horizon condition
Ask yourself why the phenomenon is limited to when the moon is near the horizon. The passage explains it.
The two images are seen simultaneously only near the horizon.
4
Relate to the first case
The passage mentions 'the first case' from earlier—squinting. See how this second case is different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو نظرنا إلى الماء عند طلوعه وكونه قريباً من الأفق فإنا نراه على التقديرين قمرين أما على التقدير الأول فلما مر وأما على الثاني فلأن الشعاع البصري ينفذ في الهواء إلى قمر السماء، وينعكس من سطح الماء إليه أيضاً فيرى مرة في السماء بالشعاع النافذ، ومرة في الماء بالشعاع المنعكس. قوله : ( وينعكس من سطح الماء وذلك لأن وضع قمر السماء عن سطح الماء كوضع قمر الماء، ولذا قالوا : لو رصد ارتفاع قمر السماء بالآلة وانحطاط قمر الماء بها في دائرة الارتفاع لوجد كل واحد منهما مساوياً للآخر، وإنما شرط كونه عند طلوعه مع أنه كذلك عند كونه مرتفعاً لأن ذينك القمرين إنما يريان دفعة واحدة حال كونهما قريبين من الأفق وأما عند تباعدهما فيرى أحدهما بعد الآخر بتقليب الحدقة والالتفات إليه.
Or when we look at the water at its rising, while it is near the horizon, we see it in both cases as two moons. In the first case, for the reason that passed; in the second, because the visual ray penetrates the air to the moon of the sky and is reflected from the surface of the water to it as well, so it is seen once in the sky by the penetrating ray and once in the water by the reflected ray. His saying: 'And it is reflected from the surface of the water' because the position of the moon of the sky relative to the water surface is like the position of the image of the moon in the water. Therefore they said: if one observed the altitude of the sky-moon with an instrument and the depression of the water-moon with it in the altitude circle, each would be found equal to the other. The condition that it be at its rising, even though it is also so when it is high, is because those two moons are seen at once only when they are near the horizon. When they are distant, one is seen after the other by turning the pupil and looking towards it.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage explains another example of seeing one object as two: the moon appearing as two moons when it is near the horizon and there is water. The author distinguishes two cases: first, the case already mentioned (squinting) gives the same effect; second, when looking at the moon near the horizon, the visual ray (الشعاع البصري) goes directly to the sky-moon (قمر السماء) and also reflects from the water surface to the eye, producing two images simultaneously. The commentator adds that the position of the sky-moon relative to the water is such that the reflected image appears equal in altitude. The moon is seen double only when it is near the horizon; when it is high, the two images are seen one after the other due to the necessity of turning the eye.

Key Terms

شعاع منعكس
قمر السماء

ارتفاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moon actually splits into two when near the horizon.
Correction: It is only an optical illusion due to the reflection of light; no physical split occurs.

Checkpoint
Why does the moon appear as two only when it is near the horizon and not when it is high in the sky?
A) Because the direct and reflected rays reach the eye at the same time only when the moon is near the horizon.
B) Because the water is only present at the horizon.
C) Because the eye is more sensitive near the horizon.
D) Because the moon is actually smaller near the horizon.

Key Takeaway
The double moon illusion is due to simultaneous perception of direct and reflected rays, which only occurs near the horizon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This illusion shows that your eyes can see two things that are actually one. When in your life have you been deceived by your own senses into believing something false about another person or situation? Will you now pause and double-check before trusting your first perception?
?

What does this say about the limits of your ability to perceive truth at once? Are there 'horizons' in your understanding of your own faith that you have not yet approached, causing you only to see partial truths?
?
If a simple physical phenomenon can fool everyone, how much more careful must you be when evaluating claims about unseen realities? What step will you take today to verify a belief you hold, rather than accepting it because it 'makes sense' at first glance?

Artificial and natural cross-eyedness: the role of habit
Distinguishes artificial from natural cross-eyedness and explains habit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between artificial and natural cross-eyedness and how habit influences visual perception.

Core Idea
Habit can correct visual errors, even in natural cross-eyedness.

This lesson examines cross-eyedness as an example of visual error. It distinguishes between intentionally induced cross-eyedness and natural cross-eyedness, and explains why the latter rarely causes double vision due to habitual adjustment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the contrast between artificial and natural cross-eyedness.
Pay attention to the term 'اعتياد' (habit).
2
Identify the two types
Locate where artificial cross-eyedness (الحول الجعلي) is described and where natural cross-eyedness (الحول الفطري) is described. Note the different outcomes.
3
Understand the role of habit
Focus on the explanation for natural cross-eyedness: why does he rarely see double? The passage says it is due to habituation (اعتياده) to aligning the rays.
Contrast this with the artificial case where deviation causes double vision.
4
Reflect on the commentary
Read the commentary section to see how the commentator expands on the idea of using the two senses in a certain position.
Consider why the commentator says 'rarely' (فقلما).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكالاً حول ) أي الذي يقصد الحول تكلفاً ( فإنه يرى الواحد اثنين) بسبب وقوع الانحراف في العصبتين أو في إحداهما ، وأما الأحول الفطري فقلما يرى الواحد اثنين، وذلك لاعتياده بالوقوف على الصواب ) وبالعكس) أي ويرى الكثير واحداً. قوله : (لاعتياده بالوقوف الخ باستعمال الحاستين بالوضع الذي يقع الشعاع الخارج عنهما من محاذاة واحدة فيرى واحداً أما إذا لم يستعمل الحاستين على ذلك الوضع، بل على وضع يعتاده من لا حول له يرى الواحد اثنين، ولذا قال الشارح رحمه الله : فقلما . قوله : ( أي) الذي يقصد الحول تكلفاً قيل : فحينئذ يكون مغنياً عن حديث الغمز في القمر، لأن ذلك من صور الحول الجعلي، وأنت خبير بأن المقصود تكثير أمثلة الغلط، فلا ضير في التعميم بعد ذكر صورة منه ولا في أن يحمل على غيره من الصور .
And like the cross-eyed (al-ahwal), i.e., one who intentionally makes himself cross-eyed, for he sees the one as two, due to the occurrence of deviation in the two nerves or in one of them. As for the naturally cross-eyed (al-ahwal al-fitri), he rarely sees the one as two, because of his habituation to arriving at the truth. And conversely, i.e., he sees the many as one. His saying: 'Because of his habituation to arriving at the truth' by using the two senses in the position in which the rays emanating from them fall from one alignment, so he sees one. But if he does not use the two senses in that position, but in a position that a non-cross-eyed person is accustomed to, he would see one as two. Hence the commentator (al-sharih) said: 'rarely'. His saying: 'i.e., one who intentionally makes himself cross-eyed' – it was said: then that is sufficient to dispense with the report about squinting in the moon, because that is a case of artificial cross-eyedness. But you know that the purpose is to multiply examples of error, so there is no harm in generalizing after mentioning one example, nor in applying it to other examples.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage discusses cross-eyedness (الحول) as another example of visual error. It distinguishes artificial cross-eyedness (الحول الجعلي) – where one intentionally strains to cross his eyes – from natural cross-eyedness (الحول الفطري). The artificial cross-eyed person sees one as two because the nerves are deliberately deviated. The natural cross-eyed person rarely sees double because he has become habituated (اعتياد) to adjusting his vision to see correctly. The commentary adds that the natural cross-eyed person uses his two eyes in a way that makes the rays fall from one alignment, thus seeing one. If he tried to use his eyes like a normal person, he would see double. The commentator remarks that finally (فقلما) he rarely sees double. There is a remark that the example of intentional cross-eyedness could replace the squinting example, but the author intends to multiply examples.

Key Terms
الحول
الحول الجعلي
الحول الفطري
الاعتياد
الشعاع الخارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural cross-eyedness always causes double vision.
Correction: Habit can allow a naturally cross-eyed person to see correctly by aligning the rays.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason a naturally cross-eyed person rarely sees double?
A) He is born that way and his eyes are always misaligned.
B) He has habituated himself to adjust his vision to see correctly.
C) His optic nerves are perfectly straight.
D) He uses only one eye.

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway is that habit (اعتياد) can overcome visual misalignment, allowing a naturally cross-eyed person to see correctly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When a truth you encounter contradicts a deeply rooted habit of thought, do you cling to the comfortable distortion or allow the truth to recalibrate your vision, no matter how disorienting?
?
If your natural inclinations have led you to a wrong belief, what practical steps will you take today to discipline your mind into a new habit of seeing—one that consistently arrives at the truth?

The Objection Against Habit as a Corrective and the Resolution
Refutes a famous objection about habit and explains the commentator's resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the famous objection against habituation as a corrective for cross-eyed vision and the commentator's resolution based on attention and habitual adjustment.

Core Idea
Habituation corrects vision by shifting attention and alignment, not by changing sensory input.

This lesson examines a famous discussion (بحث مشهور) about whether habituation (اعتياد) can truly enable a naturally cross-eyed person to see correctly. The objection claims that habituation does not alter what is seen, but the answer reveals that perception depends on the soul's attention (التفات النفس). The commentator al-Jurjani then provides his own resolution, explaining how the naturally cross-eyed person develops a habit of adjusting his gaze. This passage is a key example of how rational judgments interact with empirical habits.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: habituation to arriving at the truth does not prove that the cross-eyed sees one as one; he might still see two but believes it is one due to experience.
This is the initial challenge to the claim.
2
Examine the answer about attention
The answer states that perceptions depend on the soul's attention. When the soul sees double but knows reality, it turns away from the false image, so no double perception occurs.
Notice the key term: iltifat al-nafs (التفات النفس).
3
Consider the counter-objection
The objector points out that an artificial cross-eyed person also knows his perception is wrong, yet he still sees double. So why doesn't the natural cross-eyed see double?
This strengthens the objection.
4
Study the commentator's resolution
Al-Jurjani explains that the natural cross-eyed person can adjust his nerves to find a straight position relative to his species. This becomes a habit, so he corrects his gaze intentionally.
This resolution emphasizes voluntary adaptation, not automatic correction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وذلك لاعتياده بالوقوف على الصواب فيه بحث مشهور وهو أن الاعتياد بالوقوف على الصواب لا يدل على أنه يرى الواحد واحداً، فربما يراه اثنين لكن باعتياده المذكور يجزم بأن ما يراه اثنين ،واحد وقد يجاب بأن الإدراكات تتوقف على التفات النفس، فإذا رأت الواحد اثنين وعلم أن الواقع ليس كذلك يعرض عن إحدى الصورتين ولا يلتفت إليها فلا يحصل بسببه إدراك الواحد اثنين، فلا يتجه أن سبب الغلط موجود، فكيف لا يغلط ؟ ويؤيده ما قيل : إن ما يقع عليه شعاع البصر قريب من نصف كرة العالم، وعند الالتفات إلى نقطة لا يدرك إلا تلك النقطة، وأنت خبير بأن اعتقاد أن الواقع ليس كذلك متحقق في الأحول الجعلي أيضاً، بل هو فيه أظهر فينبغي أن لا يرى الواحد اثنين أيضاً، وتحقيق مراد الشارح عندي أن الأحول الفطري ربما يحرف العصبتين من الوضع الخلقي بالنسبة إليه فيجد الوضع الخلقي بالنسبة إلى نوعه، إذ انحراف المنحرف قد يؤدي إلى الاستقامة فمعنى كلامه أن الأحول الفطري لما كان واقفاً على خطأ حكمه بمقتضى حسه حال كونه على الوضع الطبيعي بالنسبة إلى شخصه بحرف العصبتين طالباً لإدراكه بوجه آخر مغايراً لما أدركه أولاً، فيجد الاستقامة وهذا الوجدان صار ملكة له لاعتياده بالوقوف على الصواب وكيفيته ألا يرى أنه إذا نظر إلى شيء يعتبر وضعه في النظر فمنهم من هو كأنه ينظر بمؤخر عينيه، ومنهم من ينظر بوجه آخر على حسب وجدانه الاستقامة وبه يظهر أن
His saying: 'Because of his habituation to arriving at the truth' – there is a famous discussion concerning it, which is that habituation to arriving at the truth does not prove that he sees one as one; perhaps he sees two but due to the mentioned habituation he is certain that what he sees as two is one. And it may be answered that perceptions are dependent on the attention of the soul (iltifat al-nafs). So when it sees one as two and knows that the reality is not so, it turns away from one of the two images and pays no attention to it, so no perception of one as two occurs on account of it. Thus it does not follow that the cause of error is present, so how would he not err? And it is supported by what is said: that what the visual ray falls upon is close to half of the sphere of the world, and when attending to a point, only that point is perceived. And you are aware that the belief that the reality is not so is also present in the artificial cross-eyed, indeed it is more evident in him, so he should also not see one as two. The verification of the commentator's intention in my view is that the naturally cross-eyed person may possibly turn the two nerves from the congenital position relative to him, and thus he finds the congenital position relative to his species. For the deviation of one who is deviated may lead to straightness. So the meaning of his statement is that since the naturally cross-eyed person knows the error of his judgment based on his sense while in the natural position relative to his person, he turns the nerves, seeking to perceive it in another manner different from what he first perceived, and thus finds straightness. This finding becomes a habit for him due to his habituation to arriving at the truth. Its modality is that he, when looking at something, considers his position in looking. Among them is one who looks as if with the back of his eyes, and among them is one who looks in another manner according to his finding of straightness. By this it appears that...
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents a famous objection (بحث مشهور) to the claim that habituation (اعتياد) enables the naturally cross-eyed person to see correctly. The objection argues that habituation does not change what is seen; the person might still see two but knows it is one due to experience. The answer is that perception depends on the soul's attention (التفات النفس). If the soul sees double but knows it is one, it disregards one image, so no double perception occurs. This is supported by the idea that the visual field is limited; attention focuses on one point. The objection further notes that the artificial cross-eyed also knows his perception is wrong, yet he still sees double. So why does the natural cross-eyed not also see double? The commentator (al-Jurjani) provides his own resolution (تحقيق مراد الشارح). He explains that the naturally cross-eyed person has a congenital deviation that he can compensate for by turning his nerves to find the correct alignment for his species. This becomes a habit, so he adjusts his gaze accordingly. The modality is that he consciously changes his viewing position according to his experience.

Key Terms
بحث مشهور
التفات النفس
الاعتياد
تحقيق مراد الشارح
الملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that habituation directly changes the sensory input from the eyes, making the cross-eyed person actually see one instead of two.
Correction: The passage clarifies that habituation does not alter the sensory input; instead, it involves a conscious adjustment of attention (التفات النفس) and voluntary turning of the nerves to achieve correct alignment, which then becomes habitual.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the famous objection, why does the naturally cross-eyed person not perceive double even if his eyes are deviated?
A) Because his eyes are actually straight.
B) Because his soul ignores the false image and attends only to the correct one.
C) Because he closes one eye.
D) Because his brain automatically corrects the image.

Key Takeaway
The naturally cross-eyed person's habituation works through intentional adjustment of gaze and attention, not through automatic change in perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a repeated misperception in your life—something you know is true but still see or feel as if it's false—that you have not deliberately 'turned your nerves' to correct? What specific, uncomfortable practice would it take to realign your attention with reality?
?
The passage shows that habituation to truth requires effort and intentional adjustment, not just knowing the truth. In what area of your faith or character have you settled for knowing the truth without acting to change your perception? What would it cost you to actually do the work?
?
The artificially cross-eyed person knows he is seeing wrongly but cannot correct himself. Is there a truth you know (about God, your sin, or your relationships) that you recognize but have not allowed to transform your perception because you lack the will or the means to 'turn the nerves'? Will you seek help or do the inner work to make that change?

The Spinning Disc: Sensory Blending of Colors
Jurjani explains the cause of seeing multiple colors as one due to sensory blending.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the speed of perception and the persistence of sensory effects in the common sense cause the appearance of a single blended color from multiple distinct colors.

Core Idea
Colors blend not on the disc, but in the mind's sensory processor.

Jurjani uses a simple experiment—a spinning disc with colored lines—to explain a fundamental principle of human perception: our senses can be 'fooled' by speed and memory. Before we analyze the passage, first imagine the disc: lines of different colors radiating from the center. When still, they are distinct. When spun, they appear as one. Why? The passage gives two complementary explanations rooted in the internal senses: the common sense and the imagination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and visualize the phenomenon
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Picture the millstone with colored lines from center to circumference. When it spins fast, the many colors look like one mixed color.
This is the empirical observation Jurjani is explaining.
2
Identify the two causes Jurjani gives
The passage provides two reasons: first, the retention of color effects in the common sense; second, the speed of the visual ray. Find each in the text.
These are complementary, not alternative.
3
Understand the role of the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak)
The common sense is where sensory impressions from the external senses first gather. When the eye quickly shifts from one color to another, the effect of the first remains there, blending with the second. The soul then perceives a mixture.
This shows why the blend is internal, not external.
4
Connect the visual ray explanation
The visual ray strikes all colors in a very short time, so the soul cannot distinguish them individually, reinforcing the blended appearance.
Both explanations rely on speed and the limits of perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالرحى إذا أخرج من مركزها إلى محيطها خطوط كثيرة ) متقاربة ) في الوضع بألوان مختلفة فإنها إذا دارت ) سريعة جداً ( رؤيت) تلك الألوان الكثيرة ( كاللون الواحد الممتزج ) المؤلف (منها) والسبب في ذلك أن ما أدركه الحس الظاهر يتأدى أولا إلى الحس المشترك، ثم إلى الخيال فإذا أدرك البصر مثلاً لوناً، وانتقل منه بسرعة إلى لون آخر كان أثر اللون الأول باقيا في المشترك عند إدراك اللون الثاني ووصول أثره إليه، فيمتزج الأثران هناك فتراهما النفس لامتزاج أثريهما ممتزجين، ولا تقدر على تمييز أحدهما عن الآخر، وأيضاً لما وقع الشعاع البصري على تلك الألوان بأسرها في زمان قليل جداً لم تتمكن النفس من تمييز بعضها عن بعض، فلذلك رأتها ممتزجة
Like a millstone if you draw many lines from its center to its circumference, close together in arrangement, of different colors, and if it rotates very quickly, those many colors are seen as a single mixed color composed from them. The reason for that is that what the external sense perceives first reaches the common sense, then the imagination. So if the eye, for example, perceives a color and then quickly moves to another color, the effect of the first color remains in the common sense at the moment of perceiving the second color and its effect reaching it, so the two effects blend there, and the soul sees them mixed due to the mixing of their effects, unable to distinguish one from the other. Also, because the visual ray falls on all those colors in a very short time, the soul cannot distinguish some from others, and therefore sees them mixed.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a perceptual illusion: the spinning disc with colored lines. The author first describes the phenomenon: lines from center to circumference, close together, different colors; when spun fast, the many colors appear as one blended color. Then he gives two reasons. First, the sensory process: what the external sense perceives is transmitted first to the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), then to imagination. When the eye quickly moves from one color to another, the effect of the first remains in the common sense when the second arrives, so the two effects blend. The soul perceives them as mixed and cannot separate them. Second, the visual ray strikes all the colors in a very brief moment, so the soul cannot distinguish them individually, thus seeing them as a blend. This demonstrates how sensory errors can occur due to the speed of perception and the retention of sensory traces.

Key Terms
الحس الظاهر
الحس المشترك
الخيال
الشعاع البصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the blending is due to an external physical mixture of colors on the disc.
Correction: The author shows it is due to the limitations of our sensory apparatus: the persistence of effects in the common sense and the speed of the visual ray.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the multiple colors on the spinning disc seen as a single color?
A) Because the colors physically mix on the disc.
B) Because the visual ray strikes all colors simultaneously and the common sense blends their effects.
C) Because the imagination creates a single color.
D) Because the eye is too slow to see each color.

Key Takeaway
The perception of blended colors on a spinning disc is due to the speed of visual perception and the persistence of sensory effects in the common sense, not an external mixture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that your mind blends rapid sensory inputs into a single perception, often without your awareness. Where in your daily life are you accepting a 'blended' view of a person or situation—seeing them as a single color—when you have not taken time to distinguish the separate 'colors' of their actions and words? What is one step you can take today to slow down and examine the underlying effects before forming a judgment?
?
The soul sees the colors as mixed because it cannot process them in time. Think of a recent moment when you reacted impulsively to a situation that now seems more complex. How might the 'speed of the disc'—the rush of emotions or events—have caused you to miss distinct truths? What will you do to pause the spinning next time?
?
This passage reveals that what you perceive as a simple, unified experience may be the result of hidden internal processes combining multiple inputs. Are there beliefs or feelings you hold about yourself that you have always accepted as 'one blended color'? What would happen if you allowed yourself to trace them back to their separate origins? The Mirage: Seeing Non-Existent as Existent
Jurjani addresses the objection that the mirage is not a non-existent and gives his own explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mirage serves as an example of seeing a non-existent as existent, and the two errors involved in the perception.

Core Idea
The mirage is not just an illusion; it involves two distinct errors of perception.

The mirage is often cited as an example of seeing something that is not there as if it were there. However, a closer analysis reveals that the mirage involves not one but two errors. In this passage, Jurjani responds to an objection and clarifies the nature of these errors, reinforcing the original claim that we can see non-existent as existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument. Note the objection and Jurjani's response.
Pay attention to the two errors he distinguishes.
2
Understand the objection
Identify what the objector claims: that the mirage is not absolutely non-existent, but a real phenomenon. How does this challenge the original example?
The objector says it's a confusion of a thing with its like.
3
Analyze the two errors
Jurjani identifies two errors: seeing the mirage itself (which is non-existent) and seeing it as water (confusing one imaginary thing with another). Work through each and see how they relate to the original claim.
The first error is seeing a non-existent entity; the second is misidentifying it.
4
Connect to the lesson's core idea
Reflect on how this analysis supports the idea that we can see a non-existent as existent. The mirage example is more nuanced than it appears.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) نرى ( المعدوم موجوداً كالسراب ) قيل هذا من اشتباه الشيء بمثله، فإن السراب ليس معدوماً مطلقاً، بل هو شيء يتراءى للبصر بسبب ترجرج الشعاع البصري المنعكس عن أرض سبخة كما ينعكس من الماء، فيحسب لذلك ماء. وعندي أن في السراب غلطين أحدهما رؤية نفسه فإنه أمر مخيل وليس في الخارج إلا الشعاع المترجرج وسبب تخيله ترجرجه كما اعترف به صاحب القيل فهو معدوم من حيث ذاته ويحسب الناظر أنه موجود وهو الذي قصده المصنف ولذا لم يقل : كالسراب يرى ماء وثانيهما رؤيته ماء، وهذا ما ذكره صاحب القيل وهو من اشتباه مخيل بمخيل مثله إذ ليس شيء من السراب والماء موجوداً، ولك أن تقول : معنى كلام المصنف كالسراب كما في السراب فإنه يرى الماء المعدوم موجودا.
And we see the non-existent as existent, like the mirage. It is said: This is from the confusion of a thing with its like, for the mirage is not absolutely non-existent, but rather it is something that appears to the sight due to the trembling of the visual ray reflected from a salty ground, just as it is reflected from water, so it is taken for water. In my view, there are two errors in the mirage: one is seeing itself, for it is an imaginary thing and nothing exists externally except the trembling ray, and the cause of its imagination is its trembling, as the author of the objection admitted; so it is non-existent in its essence, and the observer thinks it is existent, which is what the author meant, and therefore he did not say 'like the mirage seeing water'. The second is seeing it as water, and this is what the objector mentioned, and it is from the confusion of an imaginary thing with another imaginary thing like it, since neither the mirage nor the water exists. And you could say: The meaning of the author's words is 'like the mirage, as in the mirage, for one sees the non-existent water as existent'.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage deals with the mirage as an example of seeing something non-existent as existent. The author first presents the phenomenon and then an objection: the mirage is not absolutely non-existent; it is a real optical phenomenon caused by trembling rays. The objector says it is a case of confusing one thing with another. The commentator then offers his own analysis, claiming there are two errors. First, the mirage itself is imagined; in reality, only the trembling ray exists externally. The imagined mirage is non-existent in itself, and the observer mistakenly thinks it exists. That is seeing a non-existent as existent. Second, the observer sees it as water, which is also an error because water is not actually there. This second error is confusing one imaginary thing with another. The commentator supports the author's original classification of the mirage as seeing something non-existent.

Key Terms
السراب
الشعاع المترجرج
المخيل
الاشتباه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the mirage is merely an error of judgment, but the author distinguishes between seeing a non-existent entity itself and misidentifying it.
Correction: The mirage involves two errors: first, seeing a non-existent mirage as existent, and second, misidentifying it as water.

Checkpoint

A) One: seeing water where there is none.
B) Two: seeing a non-existent mirage and then seeing it as water.
C) One: confusing the mirage with water.

Key Takeaway
The mirage exemplifies seeing a non-existent as existent through two distinct errors: imagining a non-existent entity and then misidentifying it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Illusions of Speed and Substitution
Jurjani explains illusions caused by sleight of hand and rapid motion of a falling drop.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how sensory illusions arise from speed and substitution, as illustrated by sleight of hand and the line of a falling drop.

Core Idea
Speed and similarity trick the mind into seeing what is not there.

Jurjani continues his discussion of perceptual errors by offering two vivid examples: the illusions of a juggler and the apparent line of a falling drop. These cases show how the soul fails to distinguish between a thing and its likeness when motion is rapid or when a quick substitution occurs. This passage grounds the abstract concept of error in concrete, observable phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's examples.
Notice the two distinct illustrations: sleight of hand and the falling drop.
2
Identify the two illusions and their causes
For sleight of hand, find the two causes: rapid movement from a thing to its likeness, or quick substitution. For the falling drop, note the perception of a line due to speed.
The author explicitly links both to the soul's inability to distinguish.
3
Analyze why these examples matter
Ask yourself: how do these examples support the broader claim that perceptions can be mistaken? They show that external existence is not always as it seems.
This reinforces the need for rational judgment over raw sense perception.
4
Reflect on everyday illusions
Think of other everyday examples (e.g., a moving fan appears as a disk). How do they fit the same principle of speed causing confusion?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يريه صاحب خفة اليد والشعبذة مما لا وجود له في الخارج أصلاً، وسببه عدم تمييز النفس بين الشيء وبين ما يشبهه وإما بسبب سرعة الحركة من الشيء إلى شبهه، وإما بسبب إقامة البدل مقام المبدل منه بسرعة على وجه لا يقف عليه إلا من يعرف تلك الأعمال ) وكالخط) لنزول القطرة) فإن القطرة إذ نزلت سريعا يرى هناك خط
And what the possessor of sleight of hand and jugglery shows, which has no existence at all in the external world, and its cause is the soul's inability to distinguish between a thing and what resembles it, either due to the speed of movement from the thing to its likeness, or due to substituting one thing for another quickly in a way that only those familiar with such actions can perceive. And like the line of the falling drop: for when the drop falls quickly, a line is seen there.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents two more examples of perceptual errors: sleight of hand (and jugglery) and the line of a falling drop. The author states that in sleight of hand, what is shown has no external existence at all. The cause is the soul's failure to distinguish between a thing and its resembling counterpart, either because of rapid movement from one to the other, or because of a quick substitution that goes unnoticed except by those practiced in such arts. Then the author adds a second example: the line perceived when a drop falls quickly. This reinforces the principle that speed can create illusions.

Correction: The passage states that what is shown 'has no existence at all in the external world'; it is an illusion due to the soul's confusion.

The Reason for Visual Continuity: Raindrop and Fire Circle
Al-Jurjani explains why a falling raindrop appears as a line and a spinning flame as a circle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the persistence of images in the common sense and the rapid connection of the ray cause the appearance of continuous lines and circles from moving objects.

Core Idea
Visual continuity is a product of memory, not of the eye alone.

Al-Jurjani explains two common visual illusions: a falling raindrop looks like a line, and a spinning flame looks like a circle. Behind these appearances lies a deeper principle about how perception works. In this lesson, we explore the roles of the common sense and the ray in creating continuous perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation carefully. Notice how the author presents two causes and a commentary.
The passage is from a philosophical text; terms like 'common sense' have a technical meaning.
2
Identify the two causes
The passage first gives the cause through the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), then through the ray (al-shu‘ā‘). Locate each and note how they differ.
The first cause relies on temporal persistence; the second on near-instantaneous connection.
3
Understand the role of the common sense
The common sense is the faculty that combines sensory inputs. When the eye perceives the droplet in two positions quickly, the common sense connects the images before the first fades, creating a continuous line.
This implies that perception is not instantaneous but involves memory.
4
Understand the role of the ray
The ray (shu‘ā‘) is the visual ray emitted from the eye. In a very short time, it connects with the object in multiple places, making it seem as if the ray connected simultaneously. This also leads to seeing a continuous shape.
The ray explanation is an alternative to the common sense one, but both rely on temporal integration.
5
Reflect on motion perception
The commentary (qāla) clarifies that motion is not directly seen but inferred by the estimative faculty (al-wahm) from the change of positions. If the change is sensed, the soul judges motion; otherwise not.
This connects the illusion to a broader epistemological point about how we perceive change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسببه أن البصر إذا أدرك القطرة، أو الشعلة في موضع وأداها إلى الحس المشترك، ثم أدركها في موضع آخر قبل أن يزول أثرها عن الحس المشترك اتصل هناك صورتها في الموضع الثاني بصورتها في الموضع الأول ، فيرى كما مر ممتداً إما على الاستقامة أو الاستدارة، وأيضاً لما اتصل الشعاع بها في مواضع متعددة في زمان قليل جداً، كان ذلك بمنزلة اتصال الشعاع بها في تلك المواضع دفعة واحدة فيرى لذلك خطاً مستقيماً، أو دائرة. قوله : أن البصر الخ يعني أن الحركة ليست بمبصرة بالذات بل ينتزعها الوهم عن أن الشيء المبصر، أو الملموس بتوسط اختلاف أوضاعه بالقياس إلى غيره، فإذا كان تغير الأوضاع مستفاداً من الإحساس حكمت النفس بالحركة وإلا فلا.
And its cause is that when the eye perceives the droplet or the flame in a location and conveys it to the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), then perceives it in another location before its impression fades from the common sense, its image in the second location connects with its image in the first location, so it is seen as if extended, either straight or circular. Also, because the ray connected with it in multiple places in a very short time, that is like the ray connecting with it in those places all at once, so it sees a straight line or a circle. His statement: 'that the eye, etc.' means that motion is not perceived by the eye essentially, but the estimative faculty (al-wahm) extracts it from the perceived object or touched object by means of the difference of its positions relative to another; so if the change of positions is obtained from sensation, the soul judges motion, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage explains the cause of two visual illusions: the falling raindrop appearing as a straight line and the rapidly spinning flame appearing as a circle. The author states that the cause is the persistence of the image in the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) after the eye has perceived the object in one position and then another. The image of the second position connects with the image of the first before the first fades, resulting in a continuous perception. Additionally, the light ray (al-shu‘ā‘) connects with the object in multiple positions in a very short time, which is equivalent to connecting all at once, so a continuous line or circle is seen. The commentary (qāla) elaborates that motion is perceived indirectly: it is not seen by the eye itself but is extracted by the estimative faculty (al-wahm) from the change of the object's position relative to something else. If the change of positions is sensed, the soul judges motion; otherwise not. This establishes a principle underlying many visual errors.

Misconception: One might think motion is directly visible to the eye.
Correction: The passage explains that motion is not directly seen but is inferred by the estimative faculty (al-wahm) from the change of the object's positions relative to something else.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Visual continuity arises when successive perceptions are linked by the common sense before the first impression fades, combined with the ray's rapid connection, leading the soul to judge motion and see a continuous line or circle.

In your spiritual life, are you attentive to the moments when Allah's signs arrive in succession, or do you let the first impression fade before connecting to the next, losing the continuous thread of guidance?

Seeing the Moving as Stationary: The Shadow Illusion
Al-Jurjani explains the illusion of seeing a moving shadow as stationary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the human soul can misjudge motion and rest when changes are extremely small, using the shadow as an example.

Core Idea
The shadow appears stationary only because the soul cannot perceive tiny changes, not because it is truly at rest.

This passage from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif explores a fascinating perceptual illusion: why a moving shadow looks stationary. The text explains the psychological mechanism behind this error and clarifies that the shadow is in fact constantly changing due to the sun's motion. Understanding this illusion helps us see how sensory perception can be deceptive, a key concern in kalam epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and its English translation carefully. Notice how al-Jurjani sets up the illusion and then explains its cause.
2
Identify the explanation of the illusion
Find the part where the text describes why the soul judges rest when the movement is extremely small. Note the role of the soul (النفس) in making this judgment.
The key is the inability to distinguish between two very close positions.
3
Understand the definitions of motion and rest
The text clarifies that 'motion' and 'rest' here mean change and lack of change, not physical locomotion. This is crucial for the argument.
Al-Jurjani says: 'ليس المراد بالسكون والحركة النقلة وعدمها بل التغير'
4
Reflect on the deeper point
Consider why al-Jurjani includes this example. It shows that sensory perception can be mistaken, and that rational analysis is needed to correct it.
This connects to larger themes in kalam about the reliability of the senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) نرى المتحرك ساكناً وبالعكس أي ونرى الساكن متحركاً ( كالظل يرى ساكناً) أن البصر إذا أدرك الشيء في موضع محاذياً لشيء بعدما أدركه في موضع آخر محاذياً لذلك الشيء، حكمت النفس بالحركة فإذا كانت المسافة في غاية القلة لم تميز النفس بين الموضعين والمحاذاتين، وحكمت بالسكون (وهو متحرك) أبداً لأن الشمس متحركة دائماً إما ارتفاعاً أو انحطاطاً، فلا بد أن يتحرك الظل انتقاصاً أو ازدياداً. قوله : ( الظل مرتبة من مراتب الخ فإن النور القائم بالمضيء لذاته يسمى ضوءاً، والقائم بالمضيء بغيره يسمى ظلاً. قوله: (المقصود أنه (الخ يعني ليس المراد بالسكون والحركة النقلة وعدمها بل التغير، وعدم التغير فالمعني أن الظل يرى غير متغير وجوداً وعدماً في أجزاء ما وقع عليه، وهو في الواقع متغير بالوجود والعدم بسبب حركة الشمس، وتبدل محاذاة ما وقع عليه بها وهذا مع ظهوره قد خفي على بعض الناظرين وزل فيه قدمه .
And we see the moving as stationary and vice versa, i.e., we see the stationary as moving, like the shadow is seen as stationary. The eye, when it perceives a thing in a position adjacent to something after perceiving it in another position adjacent to that thing, the soul judges motion. So if the distance is extremely small, the soul does not distinguish between the two positions and the two adjacencies, and it judges rest—while it (the shadow) is always moving, because the sun is always moving, either rising or setting, so the shadow must necessarily move by decreasing or increasing. His statement: 'The shadow is a degree of the degrees, etc.' – for the light subsisting in that which is luminous by itself is called light (ḍaw’), and that subsisting in that which is luminous by another is called shadow (ẓill). His statement: 'The intended meaning, etc.' – means that what is meant by rest and motion is not locomotion and its absence, but rather change and lack of change. So the meaning is that the shadow is seen as unchanging in existence and non-existence in the parts upon which it falls, while in reality it is changing in existence and non-existence because of the sun's motion and the change of adjacency of what it falls upon. And this, despite its clarity, has been hidden from some observers and caused them to slip.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage addresses the illusion of seeing a moving object as stationary and a stationary object as moving, specifically using the example of a shadow. The text explains that the eye perceives an object in a position adjacent to something else after having perceived it in another position adjacent to the same thing; normally the soul judges motion. But if the distance between the two positions is extremely small, the soul cannot distinguish them and judges rest. The shadow is actually always moving because the sun is moving, but it appears stationary due to this illusion. The commentary clarifies that 'shadow' is defined as light subsisting in an object illuminated by another, while 'light' is from a self-luminous source. Moreover, 'motion' and 'rest' here refer to change and lack of change, not physical locomotion. The shadow is seen as unchanging in the parts it falls on, but in reality it changes as the sun moves and the adjacency shifts. The commentator notes that this apparent contradiction has confused many.

Correction: The shadow is always moving because the sun moves, but the movement is imperceptible due to its small scale, leading the soul to judge it as stationary.

The Ship Illusion: Perception of Motion
Al-Jurjani explains the illusion of ship rider seeing shore moving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul's judgment of motion depends on perceived changes in relative positions, using the example of the ship rider.

Core Idea
Motion is perceived relative to a frame of reference.

The passage illustrates a classic optical illusion that reveals a deeper truth: our perception of motion is not always objective. Al-Jurjani uses the example of a ship rider to show how the mind mistakenly attributes motion to the stationary shore when it lacks a changing reference point. This example is foundational for understanding discussions of causality and perception in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the descriptive style.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts the ship rider with the horse rider. Identify what each perceives and why.
The key difference is the perception of relative position changes.
3
Reflect on the philosophical implication
Consider why this illusion matters for arguments about causality and the soul's judgments. The passage shows that our senses can deceive us without a fixed frame.
This connects to broader themes in Islamic thought about the reliability of perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكراكب السفينة المتحركة ( يراها ساكنة و يرى ( الشط ) الساكن ( متحركاً)، وذلك أنه لما لم يتبدل وضع الراكب بالنسبة إلى السفينة حسب نفسه، والسفينة ساكنين ولما تبدل محاذاته لأجزاء الشط مع تخيله السكون في نفسه وفي السفينة حسب الشط متحركاً. قوله : (مع تخيله السكون (الخ لعدم تبدل الأوضاع بينهما، وأما تبدل أوضاعها بالنسبة إلى الماء فلا يحس به أيضاً لتشابه أجزاء الماء، وإنما يحس التبدل بالقياس إلى الشط، فيحسبه متحركاً بخلاف راكب الفرس، فإنه يحس بتبدل أوضاعه بالقياس إلى الفرس بالحركة القسرية، ويحس بتبدل أوضاع الفرس بالقياس إلى الأرض لعدم تشابه أجزائه، وإذا لو عرض له الغفلة بسبب تفكر قلبه في شيء، أو تكلف الغفلة يحسب أن الأرض متحركة إلى خلاف جهة حركة الفرس .
And like the rider of a moving ship — he sees it as stationary and sees the stationary shore as moving. That is because when the position of the rider relative to the ship does not change in his own estimation, and both are stationary, and when his alignment with parts of the shore changes while he imagines stillness in himself and in the ship, he considers the shore as moving. His statement: 'while he imagines stillness, etc.' — due to the absence of change of positions between them. As for the change of their positions relative to the water, he does not perceive it either because of the similarity of the parts of water. He only perceives the change relative to the shore, so he considers it moving. This is unlike the rider of a horse, for he perceives the change of his positions relative to the horse by forced motion, and he perceives the change of the horse's positions relative to the ground due to the dissimilarity of its parts. And if he becomes heedless because of his heart's preoccupation with something, or he deliberately feigns heedlessness, he would consider the ground moving in the opposite direction of the horse's motion.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage explains a classic illusion: a rider on a moving ship sees the ship as stationary and the shore as moving. The reason given is that the rider's position relative to the ship does not change in his perception, so he feels still. Meanwhile, his alignment with parts of the shore changes, but because he imagines himself and the ship to be stationary, he attributes the change to the shore, perceiving it as moving. The commentary elaborates that because the rider's position relative to the water also does not appear to change (the water looks uniform), he only notices the change relative to the shore. In contrast, a horse rider feels the forced motion and sees the ground's details change, so he knows he is moving. However, if the horse rider becomes distracted, he might mistakenly think the ground is moving. This illustrates how the soul's judgment of motion depends on perceived changes in relative positions.

Key Terms
الحركة
السكون
الوهم
المحاذاة
السفينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is objectively perceived independent of a frame of reference.
Correction: The passage shows that motion perception depends on relative frames; the ship rider and horse rider perceive motion differently based on what they compare themselves to.

Checkpoint
Why does the ship rider see the shore as moving according to the passage?
A) Because the shore is actually moving
B) Because his alignment with the shore changes while he feels still
C) Because the water is moving
D) Because the ship is stationary

Key Takeaway
Motion perception depends on a frame of reference; without a changing reference, the soul may misattribute motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

But could it be that you are like the ship rider, misattributing motion to the shore while you yourself are the one moving? What illusion are you suffering from in your spiritual life? Are there areas in your life where you have ceased to perceive your own responsibility and instead blame external circumstances? The Moon Illusion: Perceiving Motion Opposite to Actual Direction
Al-Jurjani explains the moon illusion via relative motion and perceptual error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relative motion of a nearer cloud can cause the moon to appear to move in the opposite direction to its actual motion.

Core Idea
The eye attributes apparent motion to the brighter object, causing illusion.

Al-Jurjani uses the familiar example of the moon and clouds to demonstrate how our senses can be deceived by relative motion. This passage is not just a curiosity—it reveals a fundamental principle about causality and perception: what we observe is not always the reality. Understanding this helps us avoid errors when reasoning about the physical world and its relation to divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic original and its literal translation to grasp the structure of the argument. Notice the precise description of the scenario.
2
Identify the illusion described
The passage explains that the moon appears to move towards the cloud when in fact both are moving from east to west. Identify the two motions: the moon's (real) and the cloud's (real).
The moon moves with the celestial sphere, the cloud moves due to wind.
3
Understand why the cloud appears faster
Because the cloud is nearer, its motion appears faster in our vision, even though the moon's actual motion is much faster. This creates a relative displacement that the eye misinterprets.
The ray of sight penetrates a part of the cloud; as the cloud moves, the ray shifts to another part.
4
Grasp the attribution error
The eye is occupied with the brighter object (the moon) and attributes the change of position to it, making the moon seem to move in the opposite direction (eastward). This is the core illusion.
Al-Jurjani emphasizes that the cloud's motion is confused with the moon's motion in the eye.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) نرى ( المتحرك إلى جهة متحركاً إلى خلافها كالقمر) نراه (سائراً إلى الغيم حين يسير الغيم إليه ) ، فإن القمر يتحرك بحركة الفلك من المشرق إلى المغرب أبداً، فإذا كان بيننا وبينه غيم غير ساتر إياه، ونظرنا إليه نفذ شعاع البصر منا في جزء من أجزاء ذلك الغيم، فإذا فرضنا حركة الغيم من المشرق إلى المغرب أيضاً، كانت هذه الحركة لقرب الغيم منا أسرع في الرؤية من حركة القمر لبعده عنا، فيصير ذلك الجزء الذي كان قد نفذ الشعاع فيه قريباً من القمر، ونفذ الشعاع في جزء آخر قد حاذاه بالحركة، فيقع بين الجزئين قطعة من الغيم فيتخيل أن القمر بحركته إلى المشرق قطع تلك القطعة التي هي بمنزلة المسافة. قوله : ( فإذا فرضنا الخ فرض حركة الغيم إلى جهة حركة القمر ليظهر غلط الحس ظهوراً تاماً، بخلاف ما إذا فرض حركته مخالفة لحركة القمر في الجهة، فإنه يظهر حركة القمر فيه أسرع من حركته في الصحو لكن الغلط في هذا الاعتبار أخفى من الغلط في الاعتبار الأول. قوله : ( أسرع في الرؤية وإن كان في الواقع حركة القمر أسرع منه بأضعاف لا تحصى. قوله : ( فيتخيل أن القمر (الخ) بناء على تبديل الوضع بينه وبين الغيم، واشتغال الحس بالقمر لكونه ضوء من الغيم، فينسب تغير الوضع إليه فيحسبه متحركاً، فقد اشتبه على البصر حركة الغيم بحركة القمر.
And we see the object moving in one direction as moving in the opposite direction, like the moon—we see it going towards the cloud when the cloud is going towards it. For the moon moves with the motion of the celestial sphere from east to west always. So if there is between us and it a cloud that does not cover it, and we look at it, the ray of sight from us penetrates a part of that cloud. Then if we suppose the cloud moves from east to west as well, this motion, due to the cloud's nearness, appears faster in vision than the moon's motion due to its distance. So that part in which the ray had penetrated becomes near the moon, and the ray penetrates another part that has aligned with it by motion, and a piece of cloud falls between the two parts. Then it is imagined that the moon, by its motion towards the east, cut through that piece which is like a distance. His statement: 'If we suppose, etc.' – he supposes the cloud's motion in the same direction as the moon's motion to make the error of the sense fully apparent, unlike if he supposed its motion opposite to the moon's direction, for then the moon's motion would appear faster than its motion in clear sky, but the error in this consideration is more hidden than the error in the first consideration. His statement: 'faster in vision, even though in reality the moon's motion is faster by countless multiples.' His statement: 'then it is imagined that the moon, etc.' – based on the change of position between it and the cloud, and the sense being occupied with the moon because it is brighter than the cloud, so it attributes the change of position to it and considers it moving. Thus the cloud's motion has been confused with the moon's motion in the eye.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage explains the illusion of the moon appearing to move in the opposite direction to a cloud's motion. The text describes a scenario: the moon moves from east to west (due to the celestial sphere), and a cloud also moves from east to west. Because the cloud is nearer, its motion appears faster. The eye's ray penetrates a part of the cloud; as the cloud moves, the ray moves to another part, and a piece of cloud falls between the two parts. The observer imagines that the moon, moving eastward (opposite to its actual direction), cuts through that piece of cloud. The commentary elaborates that the supposition of the cloud moving in the same direction as the moon makes the error fully apparent. If they moved oppositely, the error would be more subtle. The commentary also notes that although the moon's actual motion is much faster, the cloud's nearness makes it appear faster. The error occurs because the observer's sense is occupied with the moon (brighter) and attributes the change of position to it, thus confusing the cloud's motion with the moon's motion.

Key Terms
القمر
الغيم
حركة الفلك
الشعاع
الغلط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the moon actually moves eastward in this illusion.
Correction: The moon always moves east to west; the apparent eastward motion is due to the relative motion of the nearer cloud and the eye's attribution of change to the brighter object.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the moon appear to move towards the cloud when the cloud is moving towards it?
A) Because the moon actually changes direction
B) Because the cloud's motion appears faster and the eye attributes the change to the moon
C) Because the eye sees the moon through the cloud
D) Because the moon is stationary

Key Takeaway
Our senses can be deceived by relative motion and perceptual biases, as shown in the moon illusion, reminding us to rely on reasoned analysis rather than raw perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you trust your first impression, do you ever stop to consider that your senses might be tricking you? How can you train yourself to question what seems obvious?
?
Think of a time you were absolutely certain about something based on what you saw or felt, only to later realize you were wrong. What does that teach you about the need for evidence and reasoned analysis in your faith?
?
If your eyes can deceive you about the moon's motion, how much more can your heart deceive you about your own intentions? What steps will you take today to examine your inner motives against the standard of revelation?

Objection to the Equality of the Reflected Line and Its Answer
The commentator presents and refutes a geometric objection to the equality of the reflected line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the famous objection to the claim that the reflected line equals the face's length and how the commentator answers it by clarifying the geometry of reflection.

Core Idea
Not all eye-rays are perpendicular to the mirror; only the central ray is perpendicular.

The commentator presents a classic objection to the assertion that the line from which the face is reflected is equal to the face's length. The objector argues that due to the angles of incidence and reflection, the reflected line must be shorter. The answer refutes a key assumption about the perpendicularity of rays, using the example of sunlight to clarify.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the core claim and objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Look for the initial claim that the reflected line equals the face's length, and then the objection that this is not true.
The passage starts by stating the objection is 'famous and strong'.
2
Understand the objector's reasoning based on acute angles
The objector argues that rays from the eye (except the central ray) are not perpendicular to the mirror. They strike at an acute angle and reflect at an equal acute angle. This would force the reflected line to point outside the face if it were equal in length.
Focus on the terms 'acute angle' and 'equality of angles'.
3
Recognize the commentator's counter: not all rays are perpendicular
The commentator denies the premise that all rays are perpendicular. Only the central ray is perpendicular; others are inclined. If all were perpendicular and reflected back, only the pupil would be seen.
The commentator uses an ad absurdum argument: if all rays reflected back, you would only see your own pupil.
4
See the sunlight example that illustrates true perpendicular reflection
The commentator gives an example from sunlight entering a window and reflecting from a perpendicular polished surface. This shows that only parallel perpendicular rays reflect back on themselves.
This example reinforces that perpendicularity is a special condition, not the general case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
